35:1110:95264:0:52:520:543:167:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A: (10) 
35:1111:95269:52:21:71:543:115:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:In other words, the percentage growth in small business loan originations in county c from time 0 
35:1112:95367:21:0:72:543:94:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:to time 1 can be decomposed into the sum of three components: a contribution from the Top 4, a 
36:1113:95465:0:20:72:543:708:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:contribution from other large banks, and a contribution from small banks. By definition, each 
36:1114:95559:20:21:71:543:688:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:group’s contribution to total origination growth is their initial share of originations at time 0 
36:1115:95657:21:21:71:340:667:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:multiplied by their origination growth from time 0 to 1. 
36:1116:95714:21:20:107:543:646:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:Panel A of Table 12 shows estimates of the impact of T4DS on these different components 
36:1117:95802:20:21:71:543:626:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:of to  tal  bank loan origination growth from 2006 to 2010. (The regressions in Table 12 include our 
36:1118:95903:21:21:71:550:605:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:full   suite of county-level controls and  correspond to the regression in column (5) of Table 3.) The 
36:1119:96006:21:20:71:542:584:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:first row shows that a county with a T4DS of 100% had   origination growth that was 17.9 p.p. lower 
36:1120:96106:20:21:71:543:564:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:than an otherwise comparable county with T4DS of 0%. The remaining columns show that the 
36:1121:96195:21:21:71:542:543:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:entire contraction in small business lending from 2006 to 2010 is explained by the contribution of 
36:1122:96294:21:21:71:542:522:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:the Top 4 banks. The    second row shows that   from 2010 to 2014 a county with a T4DS of 100% 
36:1123:96389:21:20:71:542:501:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:rebounded sharply, with loan origination growth 26.6 p.p. greater than a similar county with a 
36:1124:96484:20:21:71:542:481:144:Times:144:g_d0_f2:B:T4DS of 0%. However, the remaining columns show that the majority of this 2010 to 2014 rebound 
36:1125:96579:21:21:71:563:460:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:is due to other    large banks and small banks. The third row shows that, over the full 2006 to 2014 
36:1126:96680:21:20:71:542:439:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:period, there is little difference in loan growth between high and low T4DS counties, because more 
36:1127:96779:20:21:71:543:419:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:rapid loan growth at non Top 4 banks from 2010 to 2014 has  offset the initial 2006 to 2010 decline 
36:1128:96879:21:21:71:157:398:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:fro m the Top 4. 
36:1129:96896:21:20:107:542:377:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:Panel B of Table 12  looks at the group-level growth rates in  the second line of equation 
36:1130:96987:20:21:71:543:357:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:(10). In column (1), we find that the Top 4 bank  s cut their    lending by somewhat less in places 
36:1131:97087:21:21:71:539:336:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:where they had a larger initial market share. This is precisely what one should expect if county-
36:1132:97184:21:20:71:542:315:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:level loan demand is downward sloping. Specifically, since, in equilibrium, total loan quantities 
36:1133:97282:20:21:71:542:295:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:fall more and interest rates rise more in counties with a higher  initial exposure to the Top 4 banks, 
36:1134:97385:21:21:71:543:274:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:the Top 4 should find it most attractive to continue their own lending in those counties where they 
36:1135:97485:21:21:71:265:253:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:previously had the largest market share. 
36:1136:97526:21:20:107:542:232:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:Columns (2) and (3) of Panel B show that lending by other    large    non-   Top 4 banks and 
36:1137:97619:20:21:71:556:212:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:small banks gre w much faster in high T4DS counties from 2010 to 2014. Yet  , these other banks 
36:1138:97715:21:21:71:543:191:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:did not grow much in high T4DS counties from 2006 to 2010. Thus, it appears to take several years 
36:1139:97813:21:20:71:542:170:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:for   other banks to fill the gap left by the Top 4. This may be because many of these other banks 
36:1140:97912:20:21:71:542:150:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:were also   highly constrained from 2008 to 2010 at the height of the financial crisis. And their 
36:1141:98010:21:21:71:542:129:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:gradual  entry  after  2010  may  reflect external  financing  frictions  ( Myers  and  Majluf [1984]), 
36:1142:98114:21:0:71:542:108:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:whereby they are not easily able to raise the equity capital needed to expand their lending in 
37:1143:98212:0:20:72:543:708:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:affected counties. Moreover, new lenders might face adverse selection when extending credit to 
37:1144:98307:20:21:72:487:688:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:borrowers whose previous banking relationships have been severed (Rajan [1992]).32  
37:1145:98391:21:21:108:542:667:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:As above, we can also  obtain an instrumental variables (IV) estimate of  the semi-elasticity 
37:1146:98485:21:20:71:543:646:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:of  non-Top 4 banks’ loan supply. We   do so by considering the cumulative change in interest rates 
37:1147:98585:20:21:71:543:626:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:from 2006 to 2013 along with the other banks’ supply response from 2010 to 2013. These IV 
37:1148:98675:21:21:71:556:605:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:estimates are shown in columns (5) and (6) of Table 11. For example, in column (5) w  e estimate 
37:1149:98772:21:20:71:542:584:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:the semi-elasticity of supply for other banks to be 22 p.p. Again, these estimates are imprecise. 
37:1150:98870:20:21:71:543:564:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:However, the magnitude seems reasonable, hinting that the rising lending volumes from other 
37:1151:98962:21:21:71:542:543:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:banks that we observe since 2010 can plausibly be linked to the increased cost of credit in affected 
37:1152:99063:21:27:71:117:522:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:counties. 
37:1153:99073:27:20:107:309:495:144:Times:144:Times:A:D.3. The response of non-bank lenders 
37:1154:99110:20:21:107:542:475:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:Finally, in Table 13, we ask whether the credit supply shock from the Top 4 has led to 
37:1155:99197:21:21:71:543:454:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:substitution towards non-bank lenders. Because these non-bank lenders are not included in the 
37:1156:99291:21:20:71:543:433:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:CRA data, we use proprietary PayNet data.33 Table 13 shows that traditional non-bank lenders 
37:1157:99384:20:21:72:543:413:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:have higher loan growth rates from 2010 to 2014 in high Top 4 counties relative to low Top 4 
37:1158:99477:21:21:71:542:392:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:counties. We also find that, as of 2014, new alternative non-bank lenders, who   only lend via an 
37:1159:99575:21:20:72:543:371:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:online platform, have the greatest presence in high Top 4 counties. As in Kashyap, Stein, and 
37:1160:99669:20:21:71:557:351:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:Wil  cox  [1993],  this  substitution  towards non-bank  lenders further supports  the view    that  the 
37:1161:99774:21:21:71:542:330:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:declining bank loan originations in high Top 4 counties are  due to a  reduction in small business 
37:1162:99873:21:20:71:338:309:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:credit supply as opposed to a decline in credit demand. 
37:1163:99929:20:21:107:543:289:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:More specifically, columns (1) to (3) of Table 13 present the results from cross-sectional 
37:1164:100020:21:22:71:543:268:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:specifications in which we regress the percentage point change in small business loan originations 
37:1165:100118:22:36:72:543:246:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:by traditional non-bank lenders from 2010 to 2014 in county c, denoted %∆NBLoansc, on T4DSc. 
37:1166:100211:36:19:72:219:210:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:                                                 
37:1167:100260:19:15:72:557:191:42:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:32 Berger, Bouwman, and    Kim (2017) find that small businesses in areas served by large banks reported being more 
37:1168:100376:15:12:71:542:176:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:financially constrained during the crisis, but that a greater local presence of small community banks (those with assets 
37:1169:100497:12:10:71:245:164:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:under $1 billion) helps to offset this effect. 
37:1170:100543:10:15:72:547:154:42:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:33 Although lender identities are  anonymized in the  PayNet data we use for this analysis,  the  lenders are classified in to  
37:1171:100671:15:11:71:542:139:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:four categories: banks, independent finance companies, captive finance companies, and alternative lenders. We group 
37:1172:100787:11:12:71:542:128:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:independent finance companies and captive finance companies together as “traditional non-bank lenders.” Captive 
37:1173:100899:12:11:71:541:116:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:finance companies are the financing subsidiaries of equipment manufacturers who offer customers loans and leases to 
37:1174:101015:11:12:71:542:105:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:finance equipment purchase from their parent manufacturer (Edgerton [2012]). Independent finance companies are 
37:1175:101126:12:11:71:542:93:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:stand   -alone  non- bank  business  lenders.  Alternative  lenders  are  lenders that    lend  only  via  an  online  web-based 
37:1176:101255:11:0:71:111:82:100:g_d0_f2:100:g_d0_f2:F:platform. 
38:1177:101267:0:20:72:543:708:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:The estimate in column (2) means that non-bank small business lending grew by 47  percentage 
38:1178:101359:20:21:72:498:688:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:points more in a county with a 100% Top 4 share than a county with no Top 4 presence. 
38:1179:101445:21:21:108:543:667:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:Columns (4) to (6) examine lending by the new online small business lenders who have 
38:1180:101530:21:20:72:543:646:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:gained prominence in recent years. Data on lending by alternative lenders is only available in 2014. 
38:1181:101631:20:21:72:543:626:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:Therefore, we measure alternative lending in per capita terms: we examine dollar origination by 
38:1182:101727:21:21:72:543:605:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:alternative lenders in 2014 divided by 2005 population. We present cross-sectional specifications 
38:1183:101825:21:20:71:543:584:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:in which we regress 2014 alternative lender originations per capita in county c, denoted AltLoansc, 
38:1184:101925:20:21:72:561:564:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:on T4DSc. For example, the coefficient in column (5) impl   ies that per capita originations by these 
38:1185:102027:21:21:72:543:543:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:alternative online lenders are $1.84 higher in counties with a 100% Top 4 share compared to 
38:1186:102119:21:21:72:543:522:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:counties with no Top 4 banks. This can be judged relative to the mean and standard deviation of 
38:1187:102215:21:20:71:542:501:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:alternative loans per capita of $4.00 and $2.62, respectively. (By way of comparison, per capita 
38:1188:102312:20:21:71:461:481:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:bank loan originations from the CRA database are approximately $650 in 2014.) 
38:1189:102390:21:21:107:543:460:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:Again,  we  can  combine  the  estimates  in  Table  1  3  with     those in  Table 10  to obtain 
38:1190:102488:21:20:71:542:439:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:instrumental variable estimates  of  the semi-elasticity of loan supply from traditional non-bank 
38:1191:102586:20:21:71:543:419:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:finance companies. These IV estimates are shown in columns (3) and (4) of Table 11. In column 
38:1192:102680:21:21:71:555:398:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:(3), we estimate the semi-elasticity of supply for non-bank finance companies to be   26 p.p., similar 
38:1193:102783:21:20:71:415:377:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:to our estimate of the semi-elasticity of supply for   non-Top 4 banks. 
38:1194:102855:20:21:107:543:357:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:In summary, counties with a large   r  initial Top 4 bank share experienced larger increases in 
38:1195:102951:21:21:72:542:336:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:the cost of small business credit after 2008, faster small business loan growth by both smaller 
38:1196:103047:21:20:72:554:315:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:banks and non-bank finance companies from 2010–2014, and a larger presence of new   online 
38:1197:103138:20:21:72:155:295:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:lenders by 2014. 
38:1198:103155:21:21:108:111:274:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A: 
38:1199:103155:21:21:108:204:253:144:g_d0_f4:144:g_d0_f4:A:VI. Conclusion 
38:1200:103169:21:20:108:543:232:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:We    have shown that, beginning in 2008 and continuing through 2014, there was a large 
38:1201:103257:20:21:72:543:212:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:contraction in the supply of small business lending by the Top 4 banks.  From 2008 to 2010, this 
38:1202:103354:21:21:72:563:191:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:credit supply shock had a significant impact on  small business activity and employment in those    
38:1203:103454:21:20:72:543:170:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:counties that were most exposed to the Top 4 banks. Moreover, what we find particularly striking 
38:1204:103550:20:21:72:546:150:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:is the gradual nature of the subsequent adjustment process—the fact that it took  several years for 
38:1205:103650:21:21:72:542:129:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:smaller banks and non-bank lenders to  pick up the slack. And, while the total    flow of small 
38:1206:103746:21:20:72:543:108:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:business credit to affected counties largely recovers by 2014, the cost of credit remains  elevated, 
38:1207:103847:20:0:72:542:88:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:perhaps  because these  other  lenders are  higher-cost    providers.  Thus,  while  the  impact  on 
39:1208:103951:0:20:72:543:708:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:employment in affected counties dissipates by 2014, the negative impact on local wages persists. 
39:1209:104047:20:21:72:543:688:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:More generally, due to protracted nature of the adjustment process in credit markets, our findings 
39:1210:104146:21:21:71:564:667:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:suggest that a large credit supply shock from   a subset of lenders can have surprisingly long- lived    
39:1211:104251:21:20:71:184:646:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:effects on real activity. 
39:1212:104277:20:21:108:543:626:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:A:However, the  precise  array  of  forces  that led  to  this particular supply  shock remains 
39:1213:104371:21:21:72:543:605:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:unclear. Our reading of the evidence suggests that, from 2008 to 2010, the pullback by the Top 4 
39:1214:104467:21:20:72:557:584:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:reflects a  response to the financial crisis and a reassessment of the ir  comparative advantage in 
39:1215:104567:20:21:71:543:564:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:small business lending. But there are  als o hints that heightened post-crisis regulation may help 
39:1216:104666:21:21:71:543:543:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:explain why the Top 4 were slow to return to small business lending after 2010, even as smaller 
39:1217:104762:21:21:71:543:522:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:banks and non-bank lenders were rapidly expanding in this segment. Going forward, it will be 
39:1218:104855:21:20:71:543:501:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:useful to better disentangle the causes of this shock. For example, if regulation played an important 
39:1219:104956:20:21:72:581:481:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:role from 2010 to 2014, then understanding the specific rules that contributed the    most    would be 
39:1220:105059:21:0:72:645:460:144:g_d0_f2:144:g_d0_f2:B:helpful from a policy perspective. We hope to investigate these questions in future work.
